Box 23-141, Taipei 10617, Taiwan; Physik-Department, Technische Universit\u00e4t M\u00fcnchen, James-Franck-Stra\u00dfe 1, D-85748 Garching, Germany), AC(Kavli Institute for the Physics and Mathematics of the Universe (Kavli IPMU, WPI), University of Tokyo, Chiba 277-8583, Japan; Department of Physics, University of Tokyo, 7-3-1 Hongo, Bunkyo-ku, Tokyo 113-0033, Japan; Research Center for the Early Universe, University of Tokyo, Tokyo 113-0033, Japan), AD(Kavli Institute for the Physics and Mathematics of the Universe (Kavli IPMU, WPI), University of Tokyo, Chiba 277-8583, Japan), AE(Subaru Telescope, National Astronomical Observatory of Japan, 650 North Aohoku Place, Hilo, HI 96720, USA)"], "journal_name": "The Astrophysical Journal", "volume": "835", "publication_year": "2017/02/1", "start_page": "L25", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: strong", "supernovae: individual: iPTF16geu", "Astrophysics - Cosmology and Nongalactic Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...835L..25M", "abstract": "We present predictions for time delays between multiple images of the gravitationally lensed supernova, iPTF16geu, which was recently discovered from the intermediate Palomar Transient Factory (iPTF). As the supernova is of Type Ia where the intrinsic luminosity is usually well known, accurately measured time delays of the multiple images could provide tight constraints on the Hubble constant. According to our lens mass models constrained by the Hubble Space Telescope F814W image, we expect the maximum relative time delay to be less than a day, which is consistent with the maximum of 100 hr reported by Goobar et al. but places a stringent upper limit. Furthermore, the fluxes of most of the supernova images depart from expected values suggesting that they are affected by microlensing. The microlensing timescales are small enough that they may pose significant problems to measure the time delays reliably. Our lensing rate calculation indicates that the occurrence of a lensed SN in iPTF is likely. However, the observed total magnification of iPTF16geu is larger than expected, given its redshift. This may be a further indication of ongoing microlensing in this system.", "doi": "10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1611.04866", "issn": "0004-637X", "number": "2"}, "Paper            <div class=\"partner-logo-alignment\">\n                <!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">Interpreting the Strongly Lensed Supernova iPTF16geu: Time Delay Predictions, Microlensing, and Lensing Rates</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Anupreeta More</span><sup>1</sup></span>, <span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Sherry H. -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">2041-8205/835/2/L25</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>We present predictions for time delays between multiple images of the gravitationally lensed supernova, iPTF16geu, which was recently discovered from the intermediate Palomar Transient Factory (iPTF). As the supernova is of Type Ia where the intrinsic luminosity is usually well known, accurately measured time delays of the multiple images could provide tight constraints on the Hubble constant. According to our lens mass models constrained by the <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> F814W image, we expect the maximum relative time delay to be less than a day, which is consistent with the maximum of 100 hr reported by Goobar et al. but places a stringent upper limit. Furthermore, the fluxes of most of the supernova images depart from expected values suggesting that they are affected by microlensing. The microlensing timescales are small enough that they may pose significant problems to measure the time delays reliably. Our lensing rate calculation indicates that the occurrence of a lensed SN in iPTF is likely. However, the observed total magnification of iPTF16geu is larger than expected, given its redshift. This may be a further indication of ongoing microlensing in this system.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/835/2/L25&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/835/2/L25&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...835L..25M\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2017ApJ...835L..25M&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2017ApJ...835L..25M\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/2041-8205/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s1\" name=\"apjlaa5977s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Occurrence of strongly lensed supernovae (SNe) has long been predicted (Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib25\">1964</a>; Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib12\">2002b</a>; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib21\">2003</a>; Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>), but they had not been discovered until very recently. Quimby et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib23\">2013</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib24\">2014</a>) reported the discovery of a strongly lensed Type Ia supernova (SN Ia) PS1-10afx with a total magnification of <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0~\u00a030, although multiple images were not resolved. First resolved multiple images of a lensed SN were reported by Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib15\">2015</a>): SN Refsdal, a core-collapse SN, that is strongly lensed into multiple images by a foreground galaxy cluster.</p><p>Although many strongly lensed galaxies and quasars have already been discovered, strongly lensed SNe have notable advantages over traditional strong lenses, particularly if they are of Type Ia. This is because of the standard candle nature of SNe Ia, which allows us to measure the magnification factor directly. While measurements of the Hubble constant from time delays (e.g., Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib33\">2014</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib5\">2016</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib35\">2016</a>) need to overcome various degeneracies including the mass-sheet degeneracy (Falco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib8\">1985</a>; Schneider &amp; Sluse <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib28\">2014</a>), the magnification factor provides important information on the lens potential, which directly breaks the mass-sheet degeneracy (Kolatt &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib17\">1998</a>) and the degeneracy in the lens potential and the Hubble constant from time-delay measurements (Oguri &amp; Kawano <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib19\">2003</a>). Indeed, several lensed SNe Ia behind massive clusters, although not multiply imaged, have been used to constrain mass distributions of foreground clusters (Riehm et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib26\">2011</a>; Patel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib22\">2014</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib27\">2015</a>).</p><p>Recently, Goobar et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">2016</a>, hereafter, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>) reported the discovery of a new gravitationally lensed SN Ia iPTF16geu from intermediate Palomar Transient Factory (iPTF). In this Letter, we present time-delay predictions and interpret SN magnifications in light of microlensing. This Letter is organized as follows. We introduce iPTF16geu in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s2\">2</a> and describe our mass modeling method in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s3\">3</a>. We present predictions for magnifications and time delays and discuss the role of microlensing in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s4\">4</a>. We calculate the expected frequency of lensed SNe Ia in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s5\">5</a> and give our conclusions in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s6\">6</a>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s2\" name=\"apjlaa5977s2\">2.\u00a0iPTF16geu</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The SN Ia of iPTF16geu has a redshift <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub> = 0.409 and is magnified by a factor of <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0~\u00a056 by an intervening galaxy at <em>z</em><sub>l</sub> = 0.216 (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>). After spectroscopic confirmation, several follow-up programs were triggered to resolve the multiple images and measure light curves and time delays. Among the ground-based follow-up, data taken with OSIRIS on Keck with adaptive optics on 2016 October 13 yielded an image quality with FWHM = 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />07 in the <em>H</em> band, establishing the presence of multiple lensed images of the SN. However, only two brighter SN images were visible because light from the host galaxy dominated the emission at near-infrared (NIR) wavelengths. Subsequent high-resolution images taken by the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) in the optical clearly revealed four SN images (DD 14862, PI: Goobar). In this Letter, we use the <em>HST</em> image taken on 2016 October 28 since it is deeper than images taken at previous epochs. We choose the F814W band which shows all SN images clearly (labeled A\u2013D in descending order of their fluxes; see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f1\">1</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa5977f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa5977f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0(a) <em>HST</em> image (F814W) of iPTF16geu (2016 October 28). Lens mass models from (b) <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> and (c) <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> and normalized residual images (e) and (f) in the bottom row, respectively. (d) The reconstructed surface brightness distribution of the SN host galaxy from the most-probable lens model of <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span>. Caustics (red curves) and the location of SN (blue star) are also shown. Images are 2'' on the side, except for panel (d) which is 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 and all scale bars correspond to 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa5977f1\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa5977f1\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s3\" name=\"apjlaa5977s3\">3.\u00a0Lens Mass Modeling</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We model iPTF16geu with two different mass modeling software: <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib18\">2010</a>) and <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> (Suyu &amp; Halkola <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib30\">2010</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib32\">2012</a>). This work is done independently by different coauthors, providing cross validation of our model results and predictions. Our cosmology is set to \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub> = 0.32, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.68, and <em>h</em> = 0.72. The corresponding time-delay distance for the lens system is <em>D</em><sub>\u0394<em>t</em></sub> = 1920 Mpc.</p><p>The four SN images in iPTF16geu are almost equidistant from each other in a cross-like configuration where we expect the multiple images to be magnified by similar factors unlike what we see in iPTF16geu. Since the fluxes can be affected due to effects such as microlensing and time delay, we model each supernova image as a point-spread function with a free amplitude in the image plane. Additional data constraints come from the extended host galaxy, which is lensed into almost an Einstein ring. Both the software model the light of foreground lens galaxy with a S\u00e9rsic profile (S\u00e9rsic <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib29\">1968</a>), but differ in their assumptions about the mass profile of the lens and the model for the SN host galaxy.</p><h3 id=\"apjlaa5977s3-1\" name=\"apjlaa5977s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Parametric Source Model</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We fit the arbitrary SN fluxes simultaneously as we fit the SN positions and the lensed host (S\u00e9rsic) with lens mass model using <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span>. The lens mass distribution is modeled as a singular isothermal ellipsoid (SIE). We imposed the following constraints on the lens parameters. The centroid, axis ratio (<em>q</em><sub>m</sub>), and position angle (PA, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub><em>e</em></sub>) are assumed to be the same for the mass density and light profiles. External shear (<em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub>) is often degenerate with the ellipticity of the mass distribution. Hence, we did not include any external shear and were able to find a good model fit (see the middle column in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f1\">1</a>). We used a custom <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> (Foreman-Mackey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib10\">2013</a>) wrapper around <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> to sample the posterior distribution of our models using the Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) approach.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjlaa5977s3-2\" name=\"apjlaa5977s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Pixelated Source Model</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>With <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span>, we fit the SN images on the image plane simultaneously with its host galaxy surface brightness that is modeled on a grid of pixels on the source plane (Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib31\">2006</a>). We use a power-law mass distribution for the lensing galaxy (e.g., Barkana <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib1\">1998</a>), with six parameters: centroid (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>m1</sub>, <em>\u03b8</em><sub>m2</sub>), <em>q</em><sub>m</sub>, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>e</sub>, Einstein radius <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> and radial mass profile slope <em>\u03b3</em>' corresponding to the three-dimensional mass density <em>\u03c1</em>\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>r</em><sup>\u2212<em>\u03b3</em>'</sup>. We also test SIE model for comparison with results of <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span>. We further consider a lens model that includes an external shear component, with the shear magnitude (<em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub>) and angle (<em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>ext</sub>) as two additional parameters. The lensed arcs of the SN host galaxy and the fitted SN image positions provide constraints on the lens mass parameters. The SN and lens mass/light parameters have uniform priors. We sample all the model parameters using either <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> or the MCMC method described in Dunkley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib7\">2005</a>).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s4\" name=\"apjlaa5977s4\">4.\u00a0Results and Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f1\">1</a>, we show images of our most-probable models (top row\u2014panels (b) and (c)) and the corresponding residuals (model subtracted from the data, normalized by the estimated pixel uncertainties; bottom row\u2014panels (e) and (f)). The pixelated reconstruction of the SN host galaxy on the source plane from <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> is also shown (panel (d)) with the location of the SN (star). Parts of the host galaxy and the SN lie within the astroid caustic (red curve) and are thus quadruply imaged, whereas other parts are doubly imaged.</p><p>We also present our modeling results from both <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> and <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977t1\">1</a> and they agree reasonably well. The median of the posterior distributions and their 68% confidence levels for the lens parameters are given. The slope of the density profile is consistent with isothermal (<em>\u03b3</em>' = 2.0) within the uncertainties. The power-law models with and without <em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub> are the same within the uncertainties suggesting that the role of <em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub> is not significant. We give the convergence (<em>\u03ba</em>), which is the surface mass density in the units of critical surface mass density and shear values at the location of SN images A, B, C, and D in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977t1\">1</a>. We also give predictions for magnifications and relative time delays. Negative magnifications imply opposite parity of the images and that they correspond to saddle points in the time-delay surface (e.g., Blandford &amp; Narayan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib3\">1986</a>). As expected, the predicted relative magnifications are comparable (within a factor of 2) for all SN images. Light from image B arrives first, followed by images D, A and C consistently for all our models. Images A and C are saddle images (negative parity) and images B and D are minima (positive parity). We find that the time delays (relative to image B) are within a day, a more stringent limit than the 100 hr range predicted by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjlaa5977t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nModel PL+<em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"right\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" type=\"math/tex\">{4.7}_{-1.2}^{+1.3}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"right\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span type=\"math/tex\">-{3.6}_{-1.3}^{+1.2}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"right\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977ieqn29.gif\" alt=\"${7.4}_{-2.0}^{+1.9}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{7.4}_{-2.0}^{+1.9}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22610</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.7\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.4\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong> <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> is the Einstein radius. <em>q</em><sub>m</sub> is the axis ratio of the lens mass. The PAs (<em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub><em>e</em></sub> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977ieqn30.gif\" alt=\"${\\varphi }_{\\mathrm{ext}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\varphi }_{\\mathrm{ext}}</script></span></span>) are in degrees measured east of north. A shear angle of <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>ext</sub> = 0 corresponds to shearing of the lens system along the north\u2013south direction. The most-probable convergence (<em>\u03ba</em>) and shear (<em>\u03b3</em>) values are given at the location of each SN image. Negative magnification (<em>\u03bc</em>) means opposite parity and \u0394<em>t</em> is time delay relative to image B.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/suppdata/apjlaa5977t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/suppdata/apjlaa5977t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Now, we use the standard candle nature of SN Ia to our advantage in understanding expected and observed SN fluxes. We take the best-fit Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) i-band model light curve from <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a> (corresponding to F814W). After accounting for time dilation and dust extinction of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977ieqn31.gif\" alt=\"$E(B-V)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">E(B-V)</script></span></span> = 0.31 assuming <em>Rv</em> = 3.1, we calculate the unlensed SN flux expected on 2016 October 28 (35 days from maximum) to be 24.21 ABmag. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f2\">2</a>, we show the unlensed SN flux scaled up by magnification factors from our models (filled symbols). For comparison, we also plot the observed PSF fluxes of the SN images (open symbols, based on fits by the two modeling software). It is interesting to note that image D, which appears the faintest in the data, is predicted to have the largest magnification factor. Image C is the least magnified of all and most consistent with expected magnification. Both images A and B are expected to be fainter than D in the absence of any external factors affecting the fluxes, but are found to be greatly magnified.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa5977f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa5977f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Fluxes of SN images A, B, C, and D. Expected fluxes after scaling the intrinsic SN flux (24.21 ABmag, dashed line) by the lens-model magnification (<em>\u03bc</em>) factors (filled symbols) are compared with PSF model fluxes fit to the <em>HST</em> image. Relative magnifications are more robust than the absolute values across different models. Fluxes of most of the images depart from predictions. Image A is the most magnified and image D appears to be suppressed (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s4\">4</a> for further discussion).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa5977f2\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa5977f2\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Since the relative time delays are less than a day from our models, differences in the observed fluxes are unlikely to be due to time delays. While differential dust extinction could be a possible cause of anomalies in fluxes, it is unlikely to produce such large differences, given the almost symmetrical distribution of SN images around the lens, which is an early-type galaxy (e.g., Falco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib9\">1999</a>; El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib14\">2006</a>, also, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a> suggest that extinction is not significant). The most likely explanation for highly anomalous fluxes is microlensing due to stars in the foreground lens galaxy.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977fn1\">8</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>Saddle images are more susceptible to show microlensing (de-)magnification in their fluxes, whereas minimum images typically have enhanced fluxes over the macro-magnification (i.e., magnification from a smooth mass model; e.g., Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib16\">2006</a>). Indeed, we find that image A, located at a saddle point, is brighter than our smooth mass model predictions by nearly two magnitudes for the epoch presented here. Images B and D, which are at minima, are found to be magnified and suppressed, respectively, by nearly a magnitude each. Image C does not seem to be affected by microlensing. However, we need to analyze multi-epoch data in order to be certain about the extent to which each of the images are affected by microlensing. This work is left to a future paper.</p><p>Dobler &amp; Keeton (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib6\">2006</a>) calculated the optical depths for microlensing of lensed SN Ia and found a high probability for lensed SNe to be affected by microlensing. Using typical stellar mass fractions measured from the lens galaxies of the Sloan ACS Survey (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib4\">2004</a>), they found that 25% of lensed SN Ia showed differences of more than 1 magnitude. The stellar mass fraction (&lt;<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>) for the lens in iPTF16geu is ~0.9 derived using stellar mass\u2014velocity dispersion (<em>M</em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lowast.gif\" alt=\"lowast\" align=\"baseline\" />\u2013<em>\u03c3</em>) relation (Zahid et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib36\">2016</a> and references therein). This suggests a high optical depth, although there is a large uncertainty in the mass fraction due to the scatter (about a factor of 2) in the <em>M</em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lowast.gif\" alt=\"lowast\" align=\"baseline\" />\u2013<em>\u03c3</em> relation. For a typical SN photosphere size (10<sup>15</sup> cm) and lens velocity dispersion (~150 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>), we calculate caustic crossing time to be ~2\u00a0years (e.g., Treyer &amp; Wambsganss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib34\">2004</a>). On the other hand, SN photosphere velocities of 15,000 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> imply that microlensing effects could be visible on shorter timescales corresponding to the light curve. Microlensing can affect SN Ia light curves on different timescales and produce different qualitative signatures (e.g., large magnitude offsets, introduce non-intrinsic peaks, change the decay rate), thus making it difficult to measure time delays with accuracy better than a few days (Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib6\">2006</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s5\" name=\"apjlaa5977s5\">5.\u00a0Frequency of Lensed SN Ia</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a> used the SNOC Monte Carlo package (Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib11\">2002a</a>) to calculate the expected number of lensed SNe Ia with high magnification and found that it appears to be too low to explain the discovery of iPTF16geu. Based on this comparison, they argued that lensing by sub-kiloparsec structures may have been greatly underestimated. Here, we present an independent comparison of the expected number and property of strongly lensed SN Ia with iPTF16geu.</p><p>Our estimate of the expected lensing rate is based on Monte Carlo simulations presented in Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>), in which realistic population of lensing galaxies and the source population has been considered, and various selection biases such as magnification bias and K-correction have been properly taken into account. Mass distributions of lensing galaxies are assumed to follow the SIE with an external shear. Here, the velocity function of galaxies of all types directly measured in SDSS (Bernardi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib2\">2010</a>) is used for the abundance of lensing galaxies. However, calculations of Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>) assumed that multiple images be resolved in surveys, which was not the case for iPTF. The poor spatial resolution of iPTF makes most of multiply imaged SNe blended. In order to compute lensing rates in such a situation, we use the total magnification, rather than magnifications of individual images used in Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>), to compute the magnification bias. We also impose no lower limit on the image separation (see also Quimby et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib24\">2014</a>).</p><p>In order to compute the expected total number of lensed SNe Ia, we need to know the total survey volume of iPTF. According to <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>, the total monitoring time and the average solid angle of iPTF and PTF (precursor survey to iPTF) translates into the total survey volume of ~5000\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> years, which we adopt in the following calculations. The detection limit is <em>R</em>\u00a0~\u00a021, but in order to observe light curves well we assume that peak magnitudes of SNe be one magnitude brighter than the limiting magnitude in order to be detected and studied in iPTF, i.e., the limiting magnitude of the peak SN brightness of <em>R</em> = 20.</p><p>We find that the expected number of lensed SNe Ia in iPTF and PTF calculated using the setup above is 0.9, which would be consistent with the discovery of iPTF16geu. The probability distribution for the expected redshifts, image separation, and magnifications are shown using orange histograms and compared to iPTF16geu (vertical dashed line) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f3\">3</a>. Because of the large effect of the magnification bias, our calculation also predicts a high fraction of quadruple lenses, ~65%, which is consistent with iPTF16geu being a quadruple lens. However, the efficiency of the spectroscopic typing of iPTF events goes down at <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.41 (A. Goobar 2016, private communication). The expected number of lensed SNe Ia with <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.41 is 0.16 (see green histograms for distribution of properties), making it less likely to be discovered. The observed magnification of iPTF16geu in this case also appears inconsistent given the distribution. This is in agreement with <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>, who require extreme assumptions about the fraction of compact objects in halos. This may be further evidence for the presence of microlensing.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa5977f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa5977f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Expected distributions of the lens redshift <em>z</em><sub>l</sub> (upper left), source (SN), redshift <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub> (upper right), image separation <em>\u03b8</em> (lower left), and total magnification <em>\u03bc</em><sub>tot</sub> (lower right), in iPTF and PTF, which are computed using the method described in Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>) with some modifications to match the selection function of PTF/iPTF.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa5977f3\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa5977f3\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s6\" name=\"apjlaa5977s6\">6.\u00a0Conclusion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have presented lens modeling results for the recently discovered gravitationally lensed SN Ia, iPTF16geu (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>). Our mass modeling predicts flux ratios within a factor of 2 across the four lensed images of the SN. However, the brightest images A and B are nearly 15 and 5 times brighter, respectively, than the fainter pair of images (C and D) as measured from the <em>HST</em> F814W image. Differential extinction may have very little to no contribution, as noted in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>, especially at NIR. And, yet the high contrast in flux ratios appears similar at other optical and NIR wavelengths (see <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>).</p><p>Our interpretation is that most of the SN images are affected by microlensing. We derived the unlensed SN flux for the epoch of October 28 using the best-fit model light curve (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>). Multiplying the unlensed SN flux by our lens-model magnification factors suggests that, in addition to the macro-magnification, fluxes of images A and B are further magnified by more than a magnitude and image D is suppressed by nearly a magnitude due to microlensing. While image C does not seem to be affected by microlensing from the current analysis, we need to analyze multi-epoch data to understand how each of the SN images are affected by microlensing.</p><p>We predict relative time delays of the order of less than a day, a consistent but more stringent upper limit than the hundred hours predicted in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>. Accurate measurements of the time delays will require observations with high cadence (~a few hours apart) and preferably around characteristic features in the light curves. However, small time delays predicted in our mass modeling and probable microlensing effects suggest that accurate measurements of time delays may be quite challenging (e.g., Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib6\">2006</a>).</p><p>Finally, based on our detailed calculations of lensing rates, the expected average number of lensed SN Ia from PTF/iPTF is 0.9. However the spectroscopic follow-up and typing efficiency implies a restriction of <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.41 (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>; A. Goobar 2016, private communication), which reduces this expected number to 0.16. This also implies that the high observed magnification of iPTF16geu is quite unlikely, and hints at a possible role of microlensing.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank Ariel Goobar, Sergey Blinnikov, Alexey Tolstov, and Matteo Barnab\u00e8 for helpful discussions and the referee for useful suggestions. This work is supported by World Premier International Research Center Initiative (WPI Initiative), MEXT, Japan. M.O. is supported by JSPS KAKENHI grant number 26800093 and 15H05892. S.H.S. gratefully acknowledges support from the Max Planck Society through the Max Planck Research Group. Based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>, obtained from the data archive at the Space Telescope Science Institute. STScI is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc. under NASA contract NAS 5-26555.</p><p><em>Facility: HST</em>(WFC3).</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjlaa5977fn1\" id=\"apjlaa5977fn1\">8\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We do not consider lensing by subhalo or millilensing explicitly because it essentially has the same consequences qualitatively as microlensing in single epoch images, but arising due to a more massive subhalo instead of stars. With superior resolution and multi-epoch data, it may be possible to disentangle between millilensing (static) and microlensing (time-varying) effects. We restrict our discussion to microlensing for iPTF16geu which we believe has a higher optical depth, but we acknowledge the possible presence of millilensing.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon exp-w-hash references\">\n <main role=\"main\">\n        <!-- Secondary header starts -->\n<div class=\"secondary-header cf\" id=\"wd-secondary-header\">\n    <!-- Branded journal header starts -->\n<div class=\"big-branded\">\n\n    <div class=\"publication-name\" id=\"wd-pub-name\">\n\t<div class=\"publication-title\" itemprop=\"name\" itemid=\"periodical\">\n\t\t\t\t<!-- Journal image link tile starts -->\n<!-- Logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"/journal/2041-8205\" itemprop=\"url\">\n    <img src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/9232b396-d09c-11e5-b0b6-759f86a2008e/apjl-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Astrophysical Journal Letters\">\n</a>\n<!-- Logo tile ends -->\n<!-- Journal image link tile ends -->\n</div>\n\t\t</div>\n    <div class=\"partner-logos m-hide\" id=\"wd-partner-logos\">\n            <div class=\"partner-logo-alignment\">\n                <!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/8ed669b2-d09c-11e5-b0b6-759f86a2008e/aas-2018.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The American Astronomical Society, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/8ed669b2-d09c-11e5-b0b6-759f86a2008e/aas-2018.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The American Astronomical Society, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">Interpreting the Strongly Lensed Supernova iPTF16geu: Time Delay Predictions, Microlensing, and Lensing Rates</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Anupreeta More</span><sup>1</sup></span>, <span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Sherry H. End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D2041-8205%2F835%2F2%2FL25\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">2041-8205/835/2/L25</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>We present predictions for time delays between multiple images of the gravitationally lensed supernova, iPTF16geu, which was recently discovered from the intermediate Palomar Transient Factory (iPTF). As the supernova is of Type Ia where the intrinsic luminosity is usually well known, accurately measured time delays of the multiple images could provide tight constraints on the Hubble constant. According to our lens mass models constrained by the <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> F814W image, we expect the maximum relative time delay to be less than a day, which is consistent with the maximum of 100 hr reported by Goobar et al. but places a stringent upper limit. Furthermore, the fluxes of most of the supernova images depart from expected values suggesting that they are affected by microlensing. The microlensing timescales are small enough that they may pose significant problems to measure the time delays reliably. Our lensing rate calculation indicates that the occurrence of a lensed SN in iPTF is likely. However, the observed total magnification of iPTF16geu is larger than expected, given its redshift. This may be a further indication of ongoing microlensing in this system.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/835/2/L25&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/835/2/L25&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...835L..25M\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2017ApJ...835L..25M&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2017ApJ...835L..25M\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/2041-8205/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s1\" name=\"apjlaa5977s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Occurrence of strongly lensed supernovae (SNe) has long been predicted (Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib25\">1964</a>; Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib12\">2002b</a>; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib21\">2003</a>; Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>), but they had not been discovered until very recently. Quimby et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib23\">2013</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib24\">2014</a>) reported the discovery of a strongly lensed Type Ia supernova (SN Ia) PS1-10afx with a total magnification of <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0~\u00a030, although multiple images were not resolved. First resolved multiple images of a lensed SN were reported by Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib15\">2015</a>): SN Refsdal, a core-collapse SN, that is strongly lensed into multiple images by a foreground galaxy cluster.</p><p>Although many strongly lensed galaxies and quasars have already been discovered, strongly lensed SNe have notable advantages over traditional strong lenses, particularly if they are of Type Ia. This is because of the standard candle nature of SNe Ia, which allows us to measure the magnification factor directly. While measurements of the Hubble constant from time delays (e.g., Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib33\">2014</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib5\">2016</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib35\">2016</a>) need to overcome various degeneracies including the mass-sheet degeneracy (Falco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib8\">1985</a>; Schneider &amp; Sluse <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib28\">2014</a>), the magnification factor provides important information on the lens potential, which directly breaks the mass-sheet degeneracy (Kolatt &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib17\">1998</a>) and the degeneracy in the lens potential and the Hubble constant from time-delay measurements (Oguri &amp; Kawano <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib19\">2003</a>). Indeed, several lensed SNe Ia behind massive clusters, although not multiply imaged, have been used to constrain mass distributions of foreground clusters (Riehm et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib26\">2011</a>; Patel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib22\">2014</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib27\">2015</a>).</p><p>Recently, Goobar et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">2016</a>, hereafter, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>) reported the discovery of a new gravitationally lensed SN Ia iPTF16geu from intermediate Palomar Transient Factory (iPTF). In this Letter, we present time-delay predictions and interpret SN magnifications in light of microlensing. This Letter is organized as follows. We introduce iPTF16geu in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s2\">2</a> and describe our mass modeling method in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s3\">3</a>. We present predictions for magnifications and time delays and discuss the role of microlensing in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s4\">4</a>. We calculate the expected frequency of lensed SNe Ia in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s5\">5</a> and give our conclusions in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s6\">6</a>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s2\" name=\"apjlaa5977s2\">2.\u00a0iPTF16geu</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The SN Ia of iPTF16geu has a redshift <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub> = 0.409 and is magnified by a factor of <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0~\u00a056 by an intervening galaxy at <em>z</em><sub>l</sub> = 0.216 (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>). After spectroscopic confirmation, several follow-up programs were triggered to resolve the multiple images and measure light curves and time delays. Among the ground-based follow-up, data taken with OSIRIS on Keck with adaptive optics on 2016 October 13 yielded an image quality with FWHM = 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />07 in the <em>H</em> band, establishing the presence of multiple lensed images of the SN. However, only two brighter SN images were visible because light from the host galaxy dominated the emission at near-infrared (NIR) wavelengths. Subsequent high-resolution images taken by the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) in the optical clearly revealed four SN images (DD 14862, PI: Goobar). In this Letter, we use the <em>HST</em> image taken on 2016 October 28 since it is deeper than images taken at previous epochs. We choose the F814W band which shows all SN images clearly (labeled A\u2013D in descending order of their fluxes; see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f1\">1</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa5977f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa5977f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0(a) <em>HST</em> image (F814W) of iPTF16geu (2016 October 28). Lens mass models from (b) <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> and (c) <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> and normalized residual images (e) and (f) in the bottom row, respectively. (d) The reconstructed surface brightness distribution of the SN host galaxy from the most-probable lens model of <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span>. Caustics (red curves) and the location of SN (blue star) are also shown. Images are 2'' on the side, except for panel (d) which is 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 and all scale bars correspond to 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa5977f1\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa5977f1\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s3\" name=\"apjlaa5977s3\">3.\u00a0Lens Mass Modeling</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We model iPTF16geu with two different mass modeling software: <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib18\">2010</a>) and <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> (Suyu &amp; Halkola <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib30\">2010</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib32\">2012</a>). This work is done independently by different coauthors, providing cross validation of our model results and predictions. Our cosmology is set to \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub> = 0.32, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.68, and <em>h</em> = 0.72. The corresponding time-delay distance for the lens system is <em>D</em><sub>\u0394<em>t</em></sub> = 1920 Mpc.</p><p>The four SN images in iPTF16geu are almost equidistant from each other in a cross-like configuration where we expect the multiple images to be magnified by similar factors unlike what we see in iPTF16geu. Since the fluxes can be affected due to effects such as microlensing and time delay, we model each supernova image as a point-spread function with a free amplitude in the image plane. Additional data constraints come from the extended host galaxy, which is lensed into almost an Einstein ring. Both the software model the light of foreground lens galaxy with a S\u00e9rsic profile (S\u00e9rsic <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib29\">1968</a>), but differ in their assumptions about the mass profile of the lens and the model for the SN host galaxy.</p><h3 id=\"apjlaa5977s3-1\" name=\"apjlaa5977s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Parametric Source Model</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We fit the arbitrary SN fluxes simultaneously as we fit the SN positions and the lensed host (S\u00e9rsic) with lens mass model using <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span>. The lens mass distribution is modeled as a singular isothermal ellipsoid (SIE). We imposed the following constraints on the lens parameters. The centroid, axis ratio (<em>q</em><sub>m</sub>), and position angle (PA, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub><em>e</em></sub>) are assumed to be the same for the mass density and light profiles. External shear (<em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub>) is often degenerate with the ellipticity of the mass distribution. Hence, we did not include any external shear and were able to find a good model fit (see the middle column in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f1\">1</a>). We used a custom <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> (Foreman-Mackey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib10\">2013</a>) wrapper around <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> to sample the posterior distribution of our models using the Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) approach.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjlaa5977s3-2\" name=\"apjlaa5977s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Pixelated Source Model</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>With <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span>, we fit the SN images on the image plane simultaneously with its host galaxy surface brightness that is modeled on a grid of pixels on the source plane (Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib31\">2006</a>). We use a power-law mass distribution for the lensing galaxy (e.g., Barkana <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib1\">1998</a>), with six parameters: centroid (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>m1</sub>, <em>\u03b8</em><sub>m2</sub>), <em>q</em><sub>m</sub>, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>e</sub>, Einstein radius <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> and radial mass profile slope <em>\u03b3</em>' corresponding to the three-dimensional mass density <em>\u03c1</em>\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>r</em><sup>\u2212<em>\u03b3</em>'</sup>. We also test SIE model for comparison with results of <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span>. We further consider a lens model that includes an external shear component, with the shear magnitude (<em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub>) and angle (<em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>ext</sub>) as two additional parameters. The lensed arcs of the SN host galaxy and the fitted SN image positions provide constraints on the lens mass parameters. The SN and lens mass/light parameters have uniform priors. We sample all the model parameters using either <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> or the MCMC method described in Dunkley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib7\">2005</a>).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s4\" name=\"apjlaa5977s4\">4.\u00a0Results and Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f1\">1</a>, we show images of our most-probable models (top row\u2014panels (b) and (c)) and the corresponding residuals (model subtracted from the data, normalized by the estimated pixel uncertainties; bottom row\u2014panels (e) and (f)). The pixelated reconstruction of the SN host galaxy on the source plane from <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> is also shown (panel (d)) with the location of the SN (star). Parts of the host galaxy and the SN lie within the astroid caustic (red curve) and are thus quadruply imaged, whereas other parts are doubly imaged.</p><p>We also present our modeling results from both <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> and <span class=\"small-caps\">Glee</span> in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977t1\">1</a> and they agree reasonably well. The median of the posterior distributions and their 68% confidence levels for the lens parameters are given. The slope of the density profile is consistent with isothermal (<em>\u03b3</em>' = 2.0) within the uncertainties. The power-law models with and without <em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub> are the same within the uncertainties suggesting that the role of <em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub> is not significant. We give the convergence (<em>\u03ba</em>), which is the surface mass density in the units of critical surface mass density and shear values at the location of SN images A, B, C, and D in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977t1\">1</a>. We also give predictions for magnifications and relative time delays. Negative magnifications imply opposite parity of the images and that they correspond to saddle points in the time-delay surface (e.g., Blandford &amp; Narayan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib3\">1986</a>). As expected, the predicted relative magnifications are comparable (within a factor of 2) for all SN images. Light from image B arrives first, followed by images D, A and C consistently for all our models. Images A and C are saddle images (negative parity) and images B and D are minima (positive parity). We find that the time delays (relative to image B) are within a day, a more stringent limit than the 100 hr range predicted by <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjlaa5977t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nModel PL+<em>\u03b3</em><sub>ext</sub></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"right\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span type=\"math/tex\">{4.7}_{-1.2}^{+1.3}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"right\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span
