type=\"math/tex\">{7.4}_{-2.0}^{+1.9}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22610</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.7\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.4\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong> <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> is the Einstein radius. <em>q</em><sub>m</sub> is the axis ratio of the lens mass. The PAs (<em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub><em>e</em></sub> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977ieqn30.gif\" alt=\"${\\varphi }_{\\mathrm{ext}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\varphi }_{\\mathrm{ext}}</script></span></span>) are in degrees measured east of north. A shear angle of <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phiv.gif\" alt=\"phiv\" align=\"absmiddle\" /></em><sub>ext</sub> = 0 corresponds to shearing of the lens system along the north\u2013south direction. The most-probable convergence (<em>\u03ba</em>) and shear (<em>\u03b3</em>) values are given at the location of each SN image. Negative magnification (<em>\u03bc</em>) means opposite parity and \u0394<em>t</em> is time delay relative to image B.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/suppdata/apjlaa5977t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/2041-8213/835/2/L25\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/suppdata/apjlaa5977t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Now, we use the standard candle nature of SN Ia to our advantage in understanding expected and observed SN fluxes. We take the best-fit Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) i-band model light curve from <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a> (corresponding to F814W). After accounting for time dilation and dust extinction of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977ieqn31.gif\" alt=\"$E(B-V)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">E(B-V)</script></span></span> = 0.31 assuming <em>Rv</em> = 3.1, we calculate the unlensed SN flux expected on 2016 October 28 (35 days from maximum) to be 24.21 ABmag. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f2\">2</a>, we show the unlensed SN flux scaled up by magnification factors from our models (filled symbols). For comparison, we also plot the observed PSF fluxes of the SN images (open symbols, based on fits by the two modeling software). It is interesting to note that image D, which appears the faintest in the data, is predicted to have the largest magnification factor. Image C is the least magnified of all and most consistent with expected magnification. Both images A and B are expected to be fainter than D in the absence of any external factors affecting the fluxes, but are found to be greatly magnified.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa5977f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa5977f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Fluxes of SN images A, B, C, and D. Expected fluxes after scaling the intrinsic SN flux (24.21 ABmag, dashed line) by the lens-model magnification (<em>\u03bc</em>) factors (filled symbols) are compared with PSF model fluxes fit to the <em>HST</em> image. Relative magnifications are more robust than the absolute values across different models. Fluxes of most of the images depart from predictions. Image A is the most magnified and image D appears to be suppressed (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977s4\">4</a> for further discussion).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa5977f2\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa5977f2\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Since the relative time delays are less than a day from our models, differences in the observed fluxes are unlikely to be due to time delays. While differential dust extinction could be a possible cause of anomalies in fluxes, it is unlikely to produce such large differences, given the almost symmetrical distribution of SN images around the lens, which is an early-type galaxy (e.g., Falco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib9\">1999</a>; El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib14\">2006</a>, also, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a> suggest that extinction is not significant). The most likely explanation for highly anomalous fluxes is microlensing due to stars in the foreground lens galaxy.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjlaa5977fn1\">8</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>Saddle images are more susceptible to show microlensing (de-)magnification in their fluxes, whereas minimum images typically have enhanced fluxes over the macro-magnification (i.e., magnification from a smooth mass model; e.g., Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib16\">2006</a>). Indeed, we find that image A, located at a saddle point, is brighter than our smooth mass model predictions by nearly two magnitudes for the epoch presented here. Images B and D, which are at minima, are found to be magnified and suppressed, respectively, by nearly a magnitude each. Image C does not seem to be affected by microlensing. However, we need to analyze multi-epoch data in order to be certain about the extent to which each of the images are affected by microlensing. This work is left to a future paper.</p><p>Dobler &amp; Keeton (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib6\">2006</a>) calculated the optical depths for microlensing of lensed SN Ia and found a high probability for lensed SNe to be affected by microlensing. Using typical stellar mass fractions measured from the lens galaxies of the Sloan ACS Survey (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib4\">2004</a>), they found that 25% of lensed SN Ia showed differences of more than 1 magnitude. The stellar mass fraction (&lt;<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>) for the lens in iPTF16geu is ~0.9 derived using stellar mass\u2014velocity dispersion (<em>M</em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lowast.gif\" alt=\"lowast\" align=\"baseline\" />\u2013<em>\u03c3</em>) relation (Zahid et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib36\">2016</a> and references therein). This suggests a high optical depth, although there is a large uncertainty in the mass fraction due to the scatter (about a factor of 2) in the <em>M</em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lowast.gif\" alt=\"lowast\" align=\"baseline\" />\u2013<em>\u03c3</em> relation. For a typical SN photosphere size (10<sup>15</sup> cm) and lens velocity dispersion (~150 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>), we calculate caustic crossing time to be ~2\u00a0years (e.g., Treyer &amp; Wambsganss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib34\">2004</a>). On the other hand, SN photosphere velocities of 15,000 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> imply that microlensing effects could be visible on shorter timescales corresponding to the light curve. Microlensing can affect SN Ia light curves on different timescales and produce different qualitative signatures (e.g., large magnitude offsets, introduce non-intrinsic peaks, change the decay rate), thus making it difficult to measure time delays with accuracy better than a few days (Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib6\">2006</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s5\" name=\"apjlaa5977s5\">5.\u00a0Frequency of Lensed SN Ia</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a> used the SNOC Monte Carlo package (Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib11\">2002a</a>) to calculate the expected number of lensed SNe Ia with high magnification and found that it appears to be too low to explain the discovery of iPTF16geu. Based on this comparison, they argued that lensing by sub-kiloparsec structures may have been greatly underestimated. Here, we present an independent comparison of the expected number and property of strongly lensed SN Ia with iPTF16geu.</p><p>Our estimate of the expected lensing rate is based on Monte Carlo simulations presented in Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>), in which realistic population of lensing galaxies and the source population has been considered, and various selection biases such as magnification bias and K-correction have been properly taken into account. Mass distributions of lensing galaxies are assumed to follow the SIE with an external shear. Here, the velocity function of galaxies of all types directly measured in SDSS (Bernardi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib2\">2010</a>) is used for the abundance of lensing galaxies. However, calculations of Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>) assumed that multiple images be resolved in surveys, which was not the case for iPTF. The poor spatial resolution of iPTF makes most of multiply imaged SNe blended. In order to compute lensing rates in such a situation, we use the total magnification, rather than magnifications of individual images used in Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>), to compute the magnification bias. We also impose no lower limit on the image separation (see also Quimby et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib24\">2014</a>).</p><p>In order to compute the expected total number of lensed SNe Ia, we need to know the total survey volume of iPTF. According to <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>, the total monitoring time and the average solid angle of iPTF and PTF (precursor survey to iPTF) translates into the total survey volume of ~5000\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> years, which we adopt in the following calculations. The detection limit is <em>R</em>\u00a0~\u00a021, but in order to observe light curves well we assume that peak magnitudes of SNe be one magnitude brighter than the limiting magnitude in order to be detected and studied in iPTF, i.e., the limiting magnitude of the peak SN brightness of <em>R</em> = 20.</p><p>We find that the expected number of lensed SNe Ia in iPTF and PTF calculated using the setup above is 0.9, which would be consistent with the discovery of iPTF16geu. The probability distribution for the expected redshifts, image separation, and magnifications are shown using orange histograms and compared to iPTF16geu (vertical dashed line) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa5977f3\">3</a>. Because of the large effect of the magnification bias, our calculation also predicts a high fraction of quadruple lenses, ~65%, which is consistent with iPTF16geu being a quadruple lens. However, the efficiency of the spectroscopic typing of iPTF events goes down at <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.41 (A. Goobar 2016, private communication). The expected number of lensed SNe Ia with <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.41 is 0.16 (see green histograms for distribution of properties), making it less likely to be discovered. The observed magnification of iPTF16geu in this case also appears inconsistent given the distribution. This is in agreement with <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>, who require extreme assumptions about the fraction of compact objects in halos. This may be further evidence for the presence of microlensing.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa5977f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa5977f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/835/2/L25/Full/apjlaa5977f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Expected distributions of the lens redshift <em>z</em><sub>l</sub> (upper left), source (SN), redshift <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub> (upper right), image separation <em>\u03b8</em> (lower left), and total magnification <em>\u03bc</em><sub>tot</sub> (lower right), in iPTF and PTF, which are computed using the method described in Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib20\">2010</a>) with some modifications to match the selection function of PTF/iPTF.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa5977f3\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa5977f3\" href=\"/2041-8205/835/2/L25/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa5977f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa5977s6\" name=\"apjlaa5977s6\">6.\u00a0Conclusion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have presented lens modeling results for the recently discovered gravitationally lensed SN Ia, iPTF16geu (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>). Our mass modeling predicts flux ratios within a factor of 2 across the four lensed images of the SN. However, the brightest images A and B are nearly 15 and 5 times brighter, respectively, than the fainter pair of images (C and D) as measured from the <em>HST</em> F814W image. Differential extinction may have very little to no contribution, as noted in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>, especially at NIR. And, yet the high contrast in flux ratios appears similar at other optical and NIR wavelengths (see <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>).</p><p>Our interpretation is that most of the SN images are affected by microlensing. We derived the unlensed SN flux for the epoch of October 28 using the best-fit model light curve (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>). Multiplying the unlensed SN flux by our lens-model magnification factors suggests that, in addition to the macro-magnification, fluxes of images A and B are further magnified by more than a magnitude and image D is suppressed by nearly a magnitude due to microlensing. While image C does not seem to be affected by microlensing from the current analysis, we need to analyze multi-epoch data to understand how each of the SN images are affected by microlensing.</p><p>We predict relative time delays of the order of less than a day, a consistent but more stringent upper limit than the hundred hours predicted in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>. Accurate measurements of the time delays will require observations with high cadence (~a few hours apart) and preferably around characteristic features in the light curves. However, small time delays predicted in our mass modeling and probable microlensing effects suggest that accurate measurements of time delays may be quite challenging (e.g., Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib6\">2006</a>).</p><p>Finally, based on our detailed calculations of lensing rates, the expected average number of lensed SN Ia from PTF/iPTF is 0.9. However the spectroscopic follow-up and typing efficiency implies a restriction of <em>z</em><sub>SN</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.41 (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa5977bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa5977bib13\">G16</a>; A. Goobar 2016, private communication), which reduces this expected number to 0.16. This also implies that the high observed magnification of iPTF16geu is quite unlikely, and hints at a possible role of microlensing.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank Ariel Goobar, Sergey Blinnikov, Alexey Tolstov, and Matteo Barnab\u00e8 for helpful discussions and the referee for useful suggestions. This work is supported by World Premier International Research Center Initiative (WPI Initiative), MEXT, Japan. M.O. is supported by JSPS KAKENHI grant number 26800093 and 15H05892. S.H.S. gratefully acknowledges support from the Max Planck Society through the Max Planck Research Group. Based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>, obtained from the data archive at the Space Telescope Science Institute. STScI is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc. under NASA contract NAS 5-26555.</p><p><em>Facility: HST</em>(WFC3).</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjlaa5977fn1\" id=\"apjlaa5977fn1\">8\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We do not consider lensing by subhalo or millilensing explicitly because it essentially has the same consequences qualitatively as microlensing in single epoch images, but arising due to a more massive subhalo instead of stars. With superior resolution and multi-epoch data, it may be possible to disentangle between millilensing (static) and microlensing (time-varying) effects. We restrict our discussion to microlensing for iPTF16geu which we believe has a higher optical depth, but we acknowledge the possible presence of millilensing.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon exp-w-hash references\">\n
